## Transcript

- [00:00] Baby Kanzi was recently adopted and adjusting to life in his new home. His mother was working
- [00:06] with a language coach to learn some English, and Kanzi usually came along, though he didn't
- [00:11] appear to pay much attention. But the language coach noticed that he seemed to be picking up
- [00:15] on how to communicate just by watching his mother's lessons. Oddly enough, it appeared
- [00:20] that he was picking things up faster than his mom. For example, the phrases, "You tickle"
- [00:24] and "tickle you" meant two different things. And Kanzi's mom, she was having a hard time
- [00:29] understanding that syntax. One day, Kanzi was hanging out, playing with stuffed animals, and
- [00:33] the coach asked him to make the dog bite the snake. Kanzi put the snake into the dog's mouth
- [00:39] like it was no big deal. It was a really big deal, because Kanzi is a bonobo. He's actually a
- [00:44] language superstar. Even among the elite research primates, like Koko the gorilla, Kanzi
- [00:49] was the first ape to demonstrate that language can be acquired spontaneously through
- [00:54] observation, without planned training, and the first to show a rudimentary understanding of
- [00:59] grammar, syntax, and semantics. Again, really big deal! Especially because for years, humans
- [01:05] have been proclaiming that it's language that sets us apart from other animals. But are we
- [01:10] really alone? Turns out, that question keeps getting more and more complicated.
- [01:14] Technically, we define language as a set of spoken, written, or signed words, and the way
- [01:19] we combine them to communicate meaning. If we change that definition to include the use
- [01:23] of complex grammar, then maybe we are alone.
- [01:26] But if language is simply the ability to communicate
- [01:28] through a meaningful sequence of symbols, as I might
- [01:31] do while looking for a bathroom in Sweden, or Kanzi
- [01:34] does when she's asking for roast marshmallows, well then welcome to the club, apes!
- [01:40] [INTRO MUSIC]
- [01:48] We communicate in part by engaging our brains and bodies to make sounds that let us transfer
- [01:54] thoughts from our brain to other people's brains. But of course, language is more than just making
- [01:59] air vibrate with sound. I can communicate by moving my hands, which you might have noticed
- [02:04] I do pretty frequently, or by using visual symbols.
- [02:06] All of these forms of language allow us to
- [02:08] comprehend things we've never actually witnessed, and exchange information with each other quickly
- [02:12] and effectively to, y'know, get a job, or be a friend,
- [02:16] or use a metaphor. It's hard to imagine a fulfilling
- [02:18] life without some kind of language. Humans have nearly 7,000 different languages and
- [02:23] no matter how different they sound, we can break down their basic structure in the same
- [02:27] way, using the same three building blocks. The smallest of them are phonemes.
- [02:32] These are very short, distinctive sound units like "a," "t," "ch," "sh," like, stuff like that.
- [02:38] English uses about 40 of them. Phonemes go together to make morphemes, which are
- [02:43] the smallest units that carry meaning. This can be words or parts of words, like a prefix
- [02:47] or a suffix for example. The word "speech" is a morpheme that contains four phoneme
- [02:51] sound units: "s," "p," "ee," "ch." From there, you arrange morphemes into your language's
- [02:57] grammar or system of rules, allowing you to say the things that you want to say.
- [03:00] So those 40 English phonemes give us over 100,000 morphemes that produce the more
- [03:05] than 616,000 words in the Oxford English Dictionary, which can be arranged into an
- [03:11] infinite number of sentences, paragraphs, Wu-Tang lyrics, or Shakespearean plays.
- [03:16] And just as the structure of language starts small, so does how we learn language.
- [03:21] And we start very young. The word "infant" comes from the Latin "in fans," meaning
- [03:25] "not speaking." But as early as four months, they can recognize differences in speech
- [03:30] and start to read lips, matching mouth movements
- [03:33] with their corresponding sounds like, "ah," "e," "i," "o."
- [03:37] And even at this age, you got to watch what you say about kids in their presence, because this
- [03:40] also marks the beginning of receptive language, or the ability to understand what's being said,
- [03:45] both to and about us. Soon, that receptive language
- [03:49] blooms to accommodate productive language,
- [03:51] when instead of just understanding other people, babies start developing the ability to produce
- [03:56] words. Of course, that takes a while, but in the meantime, they get a lot of practice babbling.
- [04:00] Beginning at about four months, they start to make all sorts of sounds. Although you may get
- [04:05] a "dada" or a "mama," babbling is not an imitation
- [04:08] of adult speech. In fact, it typically includes sounds
- [04:10] from many different languages, and a stranger
- [04:13] couldn't tell if a kid was Italian or Kenyan or Korean
- [04:15] just by the sound of her babbling. Similarly, deaf babies will watch their parents signing and start
- [04:21] babbling with their hands by about 10 months. That babbling morphs into something that starts
- [04:25] to make sense, and "mama" probably really means "mama" now. Without exposure to other languages,
- [04:31] a child will actually lose the ability to both hear
- [04:34] and create particular tones and sounds that
- [04:37] aren't part of his or her household language. So someone who speaks English around the
- [04:40] house soon won't be able to differentiate between certain phonemes in Mandarin
- [04:44] if they heard them, for instance, or between aspirated and non-aspirated consonants
- [04:49] in Hindi. By the time they're mowing down the first birthday cake, most kids will be
- [04:52] entering the one-word stage of language development. They now know that sounds
- [04:55] carry specific meanings, and connect the sound "dog" to that furry thing across
- [04:59] the room. By around 18 months, their capacity for learning new words jumps from about
- [05:03] one a week to one a day, and by the time they're
- [05:05] two they're probably speaking in two-word statements.
- [05:08] These choppy sentences are kind of like a telegraphic speech -- they sound like clumsy
- [05:14] texts or old-school telegrams, using mostly nouns and verbs. "Want juice," "No pants,"
- [05:20] that kind of stuff. These little sentences make sense and they follow the rules of
- [05:24] their language's syntax. For example, an English-speaking child would put an
- [05:27] adjective before a noun: "Black cat" while a Spanish speaker would reverse that: "gato negro."
- [05:32] From there, the average kid is soon uttering longer phrases in complete sentences --
- [05:37] refusing to put pants on and demanding more crackers. Most humans hit these same milestones
- [05:42] during their language development, but there are competing theories about how our infant babbles
- [05:47] turn into complex sentences and how we acquire language. You'll remember B.F. Skinner, the
- [05:53] pioneering behaviorist who brought us learning through reinforcement. He believed language
- [05:57] was a product of associative principles and operant conditioning. Skinner argued that a kid
- [06:02] learned to associate words with meanings largely through reinforcement. So in the Skinner model,
- [06:07] for example, if baby Bruno says "mmmm" and his mother
- [06:11] gives him some milk, he'll find that outcome
- [06:13] (both the milk and the attention) rewarding, and eventually, work his way up to saying, "milk,"
- [06:18] through these learned associations and shaping processes. It's good to be understood, right?
- [06:23] But as usual, not everyone was on board with Skinner. In particular, legendary American
- [06:27] linguist Noam Chomsky argued that a kid like Bruno would never reach his full, complex,
- [06:32] sonnet-writing potential if his learning was dependent on conditioning alone. Chonsky
- [06:37] instead proposed the idea of innate learning and ubiquitous grammatical categories,
- [06:42] pointing out that while the world's thousands of languages may sound wildly diverse, they're
- [06:47] actually very similar, sharing some basic elements. He called this "universal grammar."
- [06:52] Chomsky's universal grammar posited that all human languages contain nouns, verbs,
- [06:57] and adjectives, and humans are born with an innate ability to acquire language, and
- [07:02] even a genetic predisposition to learn grammatical rules, rather than being
- [07:07] linguistic blank slates. Chomsky suggested that we're hardwired for it from day one.
- [07:12] In the end though, we're still not sure how we acquire language; however, developmental
- [07:16] research and studies of other species have given us a sense that at least some of it is
- [07:20] innate, while the role of learning and exposure is also important. So if it's true that all humans
- [07:25] have some innate capacity for language, where in
- [07:28] the brain is it sitting? We've talked a lot about how
- [07:30] function is localized in the brain, and that is definitely
- [07:33] true for some aspects of language, but while speaking,
- [07:36] reading, writing, and even singing all fall under the language umbrella, their locations in the
- [07:40] brain are a little more complicated. Consider aphasia, a neurological impairment of language.
- [07:45] People can experience lots of different kinds of aphasia, depending on whether they've suffered
- [07:49] an injury, or stroke, or a tumor, or dementia. So maybe they can speak but not read,
- [07:54] maybe they can sing but barely speak, or write but not read. The region of the brain known
- [07:58] as Broca's Area and the left frontal lobe is involved with the production of speech.
- [08:02] If i suffered a trauma to this area, I might still comprehend speech but struggled to speak,
- [08:07] although I might still be able to sing, because that's conducted elsewhere in the brain.
- [08:11] On the other hand, that falling coconut struck another region called the Vernicas Area, a region
- [08:16] in the left temporal lobe involved in the expression and comprehension of language,
- [08:20] I'd still be able to speak, but my language wouldn't make any sense, so you might find
- [08:24] me saying something like, "it was two pizza i called purple brother on the television."
- [08:29] Aphasia and other brain injuries remind us how thinking and language are both separate
